### Turn 10 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Short answer: **no — there's no power-consumption or battery-budget doc in this repo.** What exists is scattered and qualitative, not measured numbers. Here's everything we actually have:

**Power-related facts currently documented:**
- `docs/camera/oak-d-camera-test.md` is the only place with real power detail, and it's all about the camera browning out the Pi, not a system budget:
  - OAK-D-LITE `bMaxPower` = **500 mA on USB 2, up to 900 mA on USB 3** (`oak-d-camera-test.md:97`)
  - Pi 5 V rail peaks at a **~600 mA budget** before it browns out (`:102`)
  - `vcgencmd get_throttled = 0x50000` → under-voltage *and* throttling have occurred (`:95`)
  - Pi PSU is nominally **5 V / 3 A**, and even that may be under-spec (`:149`)
- `docs/README.md:23` / `network/README.md:17`: the Create 3 base powers the Pi 4 over the USB-C cable — so the **base battery feeds both computers**.
- A live `battery_state` topic exists (`/turtlebot468/battery_state`, ~1 Hz) — `ros2-over-nuwave.md:38` — but **no logged values or run-time estimates** anywhere.

So we know the camera's peak draw and that the Pi already under-volts, but we have **zero** numbers on idle/active wattage, base battery capacity, or runtime — which is exactly what you'd want for an "everything running all the time" battery robot.

### Turn 13 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Not directly — **there's no per-component power metering inside a stock TurtleBot 4.** Nothing on the robot has individual current shunts (no INA219-style sensors per rail), so no software readout will tell you "camera = X W, motors = Y W." Here's what the hardware actually exposes and how you'd get a breakdown anyway:

**What you *can* read (all aggregate):**
- **Create 3 base — `battery_state`** is the only real power sensor on the robot. It reports whole-battery `voltage`, `current`, `charge`, `capacity`, `percentage`. The `current` field is the **net draw of everything on the base battery** — base electronics + motors + the Pi 4 (powered over USB-C) + everything on the Pi's USB bus (including the OAK camera). One number, not a breakdown.
- **Pi 4 itself gives you almost nothing for power.** `vcgencmd` only reports `measure_volts`, `measure_temp`, and `get_throttled` (the under-voltage/throttle flags we already use for the camera). The per-rail PMIC ADC (`vcgencmd pmic_read_adc`) that *would* give component currents is a **Pi 5 feature — the Pi 4 doesn't have it.**
- Per-USB-device draw (e.g. the camera's actual mA) is **not exposed in software** — only the descriptor's `bMaxPower` (the 500/900 mA *max* rating, not live draw).

**How to actually attribute power — the differential method:**
Since the base battery current is the only meter, you isolate each consumer by toggling it and watching `battery_state.current`:

```bash
# baseline: robot idle, nothing extra running
ros2 topic echo /turtlebot468/battery_state --field current
# then re-measure with each subsystem togg
...[clipped 662 chars]

### Turn 20 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Read it live off robot 468 just now. Here's the snapshot:

| Field | Value | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| `voltage` | **14.50 V** | pack voltage (nominal ~14.4 V, 4S Li-ion) |
| `current` | **+0.46 A** | **positive = flowing *into* the battery → it's charging** |
| `percentage` | **0.24 → 24%** | fairly low, still charging up |
| `charge` / `capacity` | 0.47 / 1.97 Ah | ~24% of a 1.968 Ah pack |
| `temperature` | **41.3 °C** | warm-ish but normal for charging |
| `power_supply_status` | `0` (UNKNOWN) | the base isn't reporting the enum correctly; the **current sign** is the reliable charge/discharge indicator |

**Net power into the battery right now ≈ 14.5 V × 0.46 A ≈ 6.7 W.**

**Important caveat — this does *not* tell you what the robot is consuming.** While docked, the dock feeds *both* the running electronics *and* the battery at the same time:

```
P_dock_input  =  P_consumption (Pi + base + camera + nodes)  +  P_into_battery
```

`battery_state` only exposes that last term (the **net** battery current, +0.46 A). The consumption part is hidden behind the dock — there's no sensor on the robot for the dock input. So the +6.7 W is the *charge* rate, not what everything-running-all-the-time is drawing.

To actually measure consumption you have to read `current` **off the dock**, where it goes negative and equals the true load. So:

- **Now (charging):** can't isolate consumption — only see ~0.46 A net into a 24% pack.
- **Undocked:** `current` flips negative and *is* the system draw; that's when the differential toggle test (motors / camera / SLAM on-off) gives you the per-s

| Metric | Value |
|---|---|
| Discharge current | **−1.00 A** (rock-steady across all 10 samples) |
| Pack voltage | ~14.15 V (sagged from 14.50 V docked → 14.14 V under load) |
| **Total system power** | **≈ 14.1 W** (14.15 V × 1.00 A) |
| Temp | 41.3 °C (flat) |

So with **everything running but the robot sitting still**, the whole machine — Create 3 base electronics + Pi 4 + OAK camera + all ROS nodes — draws **~14 W / ~1.0 A** off the battery. That's your idle baseline. This is the number you couldn't see while docked (the dock was hiding it).

## What this means for runtime
- Pack is **1.968 Ah × ~14.4 V ≈ 28 Wh** full.
- At ~14 W idle → **~2 hours** runtime from full, *before* adding motors/driving load.
- Right now it's at **24%** (~6.8 Wh), so only ~0.5 h left undocked — good call keeping it on the dock.

## The key limitation (still true)
This is the **aggregate** draw. The −1.0 A is everything combined; it doesn't separate Pi vs base vs camera vs SLAM. To attribute it per-subsystem, I'd run the **differential toggle test** while undocked: baseline → start camera → start SLAM → drive, recording the current delta at each step. Each jump = that subsystem's share.

### Turn 44 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Here's what's actually running on robot 468 right now while it sits stationary, grouped by what it is and why it never stops. Nothing here gates on motion — the whole stack comes up at launch and runs continuous loops regardless of whether the robot is moving.

## 1. Create 3 base firmware — *can't be turned off*
The `_do_not_use/*` nodes + `create3_repub` (PID 1262, ~13% CPU):
`motion_control`, `mobility`, `kinematics_engine`, `composite_hazard`, `stasis`, `mobility_monitor`, `robot_state`, `system_health`, `ui_mgr`, `static_transform`.

**Why always on:** this is the base's onboard firmware. Even parked, it continuously runs the motor controllers (holding position), polls the IMU/wheel-drop/cliff/bumper hazards, runs the **stasis** monitor, and reports `battery_state`/`odom`. It's a safety + sensor loop — it has no "idle" mode. This is also the layer that draws on the base battery directly.

## 2. TurtleBot 4 core — launched at boot
- `turtlebot4_node` (PID 1258, ~8%) — HMI display, buttons, base comms
- `robot_state_publisher` + `joint_state_publisher` — publish the **TF tree continuously** (every node doing transforms needs it, moving or not)
- `analyzers` — diagnostics aggregator

## 3. Sensors — streaming continuously (real hardware draw)
- **`rplidar_composition` (PID 1268)** — the lidar's **motor physically spins 24/7** and streams scans. This is a genuine mechanical/electrical draw that exists *even stationary*.
- **`oakd` camera (PID 14349)** + **`depth_pc_container` / `point_cloud_xyz` / `depth_to_scan` (PIDs 14437/14439)** — the OAK sensor + onboard MyriadX stre
...[clipped 1894 chars]

### Turn 55 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Yes — it's possible, and better than I expected. All three big consumers expose a **runtime stop service**, so you can power them down when parked and bring them back *without restarting the stack*. Here's the real picture:

## What you can actually shut off (and the win)

| Component | Control | Power saved | Restart cost |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Lidar motor** | `stop_motor` / `start_motor` (`std_srvs/Empty`) | **Mechanical** — physically stops the spinning motor. Genuine battery saving. | ~2 s to spin back up + stabilize before scans are valid |
| **OAK camera** | `oakd/stop_camera` / `start_camera` (`std_srvs/Trigger`) | Stops the depthai pipeline → MyriadX stops active processing **and** the Pi-side depth→pointcloud→scan nodes go quiet (big CPU drop) | ~1–2 s to re-init pipeline |
| **SLAM** | `slam_toolbox/pause_new_measurements` (`slam_toolbox/Pause`, a **toggle**) | Frees the **~94% CPU core** — the single biggest Pi-side electrical draw | instant |

## Commands

**Park / power-save (robot stationary):**
```bash
ros2 service call /turtlebot468/stop_motor std_srvs/srv/Empty {}
ros2 service call /turtlebot468/oakd/stop_camera std_srvs/srv/Trigger {}
ros2 service call /turtlebot468/slam_toolbox/pause_new_measurements slam_toolbox/srv/Pause {}
```
**Resume (before moving):**
```bash
ros2 service call /turtlebot468/start_motor std_srvs/srv/Empty {}
ros2 service call /turtlebot468/oakd/start_camera std_srvs/srv/Trigger {}
ros2 service call /turtlebot468/slam_toolbox/pause_new_measurements slam_toolbox/srv/Pause {}   # toggle back on
```

## The honest caveats
- **It's safe

### Turn 69 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
That changed the picture — let me be straight with you: **the park command mostly didn't work.** The services returned success, but the actual hardware/compute didn't stop:

| Component | Service result | Actual effect | 
|---|---|---|
| **Camera** | `success=True` | ✅ **Genuinely stopped** — depth points went to 0 |
| **Lidar** | `Empty` (no error) | ❌ **Still scanning at 8 Hz** — motor did *not* stop |
| **SLAM** | `status=True` | ❌ **Still pinning 95.2% CPU** (flat across samples) — no CPU relief at all |

So the one big win I expected — killing the 95% SLAM core and stopping the lidar motor — **did not happen**. Only the camera (the smallest CPU consumer, ~3%) actually powered down. Net power saving from this "park" is essentially negligible, and it left the robot in an inconsistent half-state (camera blind, but lidar+SLAM still grinding).

## What we learned (the real takeaway)

**Runtime service-based "park" does NOT save meaningful power on this stack.** The services accept the call and report success, but for the two big consumers nothing actually stops:

- **`stop_motor`** returns `Empty` (no error) but the lidar **keeps scanning at 8 Hz** — the motor doesn't stop. The TB4 rplidar driver effectively ignores it (likely auto-respins / no standby).
- **`pause_new_measurements`** returns `status=True` and *does* stop SLAM from adding scans to the map — but the node **keeps pinning 95% CPU**. It pauses the *mapping logic*, not the *compute*. Zero electrical benefit.
- Only **`stop_camera`** genuinely powered down its pipeline — and that was the *smallest* consumer (~3% CPU).

So the answer to your earlier question sharpens: **saving sensor power when stopped is possible in principle, but not via these runtime services.** The 95% SLAM core and the spinning lidar are the real draw, and neither honors a soft pause.

## To actually reclaim that power you'd need to *stop the processes*, not pause them:
1. **SLAM (biggest win):** kill/stop `slam_toolbox` when parked, then `deserialize_map` to reload on resume. Heavier, but it's the only thing that frees the 95% core.
2. **Lidar motor:** the soft service is a dead end — would need to investigate the driver's standby params or stop the `rplidar_composition` node outright.
3. These nodes are **not lifecycle-managed** (the probe found no `change_state` services),
...[clipped 170 chars]

### Turn 94 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Found the real reason. The lidar has **`auto_standby = True`**, and there's **1 active subscriber** on `/scan` (that's `slam_toolbox`).

## Why `stop_motor` failed
With `auto_standby = True`, the driver **slaves the motor to subscriber count** — it spins whenever something is listening to `/scan`, and only idles the motor when subscribers drop to **zero**. So when you call `stop_motor`, the driver immediately re-spins because SLAM is still subscribed. The manual service is futile as long as anyone subscribes.

## So yes — there are two real ways to stop it:

**Option A — remove the subscriber (the clean way).** Stop the `slam_toolbox` *process* (a soft pause won't do it — pause keeps the subscription open). With 0 subscribers, `auto_standby` idles the lidar motor **automatically**. This is the ideal park because it's **two wins at once**: the lidar spins down *and* you reclaim the 95% SLAM core. That's where nearly all your ~14 W savings live.

**Option B — kill the lidar process directly.** `rplidar_composition` (PID 1268) is a standalone process launched by `ros2 launch`. Killing it stops the lidar outright, but it may be respawned by the launch system and needs a full node restart to recover.

## The catch — restoring isn't free
The blocker is the restore path, not the stop:
- **SLAM:** killing it **loses the in-progress map** unless you `serialize_map` first, and bringing it back means relaunching with its original params + `deserialize_map`. Since robot 468 is actively mapping (the explorer work), that's disruptive.
- **Lidar:** restarting needs node relaunch + ~2 s mo
...[clipped 660 chars]

## The honest takeaway
Parking saves **~2 W, about 14%** — real, but **less than the "95% CPU core" suggested.** Why: a single Pi 4 core at full tilt only draws ~1–1.5 W, and the **always-on floor dominates** the budget — the Create 3 base firmware, motors holding position, the Pi's baseline, and TB4 core nodes. Those can't be turned off, so ~12 W is roughly the practical floor for this robot while powered on.

So in runtime terms: ~14 W → ~12 W extends idle endurance from ~2.0 h to ~2.3 h on a full pack. Worth having for long stationary periods, but the lever isn't as big as the CPU graph implied.

(Note: the two measurements were at different charge levels — 24% vs 33% — so compare the **current** draw, −1.00 vs −0.85 A; that delta is the reliable figure.)

## Yes — costmap and localization read scans independently

They're two separate consumers, each pointed at whatever topic you give it. You're **already doing this on the costmap side**:

- **Nav2 costmap** (`nav2_glass.yaml:161`) takes *multiple* scan sources — `observation_sources: scan oakd_scan` → raw lidar `scan` **plus** the depth-derived `oakd/scan`. A costmap layer can fuse as many `LaserScan`/pointcloud sources as you list.
- **Localization / SLAM** (`slam_toolbox`, or AMCL if you switch to a known map) takes **exactly one** `scan_topic` param. It currently reads the raw lidar `/turtlebot468/scan`.

So the asymmetry is the key fact: **costmap = many sources, localization = exactly one.**

## Why this matters for your glass plan

Your roadmap item is `depth_lidar_fusion` → inject depth into `/scan` so glass enters the SLAM map. But because the two consumers are independent, you have a **design fork**:

| Approach | SLAM/localization reads | Costmap reads | Effect |
|---|---|---|---|
| **A — inject into `/scan`** (current plan) | fused `/scan` (lidar + glass) | fused `/scan` (+ `oakd/scan`) | Glass enters the **map**. But any depth false-positive now **corrupts the map and can break localization**. |
| **B — keep `/scan` pure, fuse only downstream** | clean lidar `/scan` | clean `/scan` + `oakd/scan` (already wired!) | Planner avoids glass; map/localization stay on trustworthy lidar-only data. **No new node needed** — you already have it. |

### Turn 214 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Researched. Here's how this maps to standard practice and to your guide-robot specifically.

## Your instinct is correct — and it's the mainstream design

What you described ("clean/smooth the map for localization, but keep the objects live in the nav stack") is exactly how production ROS 2 robots are built. There are **two independent mechanisms** that together achieve it, and people conflate them:

**1. Architectural separation (the costmap layers)** — this is the one you're already half-using.
- The **static layer** = the saved map → feeds *localization* (AMCL or slam_toolbox localization mode). Should contain *only permanent structure*.
- The **obstacle/voxel layers** = live lidar + depth → feed *planning only*. Transient objects (people, carts, temporarily-placed items) live **here and nowhere else** — they're avoided in real time but never written into the map the robot localizes against.

This is the canonical Nav2 layered-costmap philosophy: dynamic things belong in the runtime layers, not the localization map.

**2. Localizer robustness to "unmapped" objects** — this is the algorithmic half of "keep them out of the map without breaking pose."
- **AMCL** has `beam_skip_distance` / `beam_skip_threshold`: laser readings that disagree with the map by more than a threshold across many particles are treated as a likely unmapped (dynamic) obstacle and *skipped*, instead of dragging the pose estimate. The Nav2 docs note this matters specifically with `likelihood_field_prob` — the sensor model "is prone to sudden divergences in the presence of unmapped obstacles," and the `
...[clipped 4659 chars]

### Turn 215 [user] (2026-06-20)
can we update them map while navigating? for example if we dont see an object where there was one in map or if we see an object where there is no object

### Turn 216 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
[thinking omitted]

### Turn 217 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Yes — and there are three different layers at which "update the map while navigating" can happen, with very different risk profiles. The key thing to internalize first: **marking (object appeared) is easy and safe; clearing (object disappeared) is hard and dangerous.** Almost all the subtlety is in the asymmetry.

## The two cases are not symmetric

| | "Object now where map said free" (mark) | "Free now where map said occupied" (clear) |
|---|---|---|
| How it's detected | A lidar return lands in a cell the map called free | Lidar rays pass *through* a cell the map called occupied |
| Risk | Low — worst case you avoid empty space | **High** — you can erase a real wall from a single noisy/occluded/reflected reading, then drive into it |
| Standard guard | Mark immediately | Require **multiple confirmations / ray-casting consensus / time decay** before committing |

This is why occupancy grids use **log-odds Bayesian updates**: each "hit" raises a cell's occupancy probability, each "miss" (a ray passing through) lowers it, and a cell only flips after *enough evidence*. That hysteresis is exactly what stops one bad frame from deleting a wall.

## Three layers where you can do it

**1. You're already doing it — the costmap (ephemeral, per-run).**
Your Nav2 obstacle/voxel layer already updates live while navigating: it **marks** new obstacles as the robot sees them and **clears** them via `raytrace_max_range` when later rays pass through. This handles *both* of your cases — for the duration of the run — **without ever touching the saved map or your localization input.** For a g
...[clipped 2845 chars]

### Turn 218 [user] (2026-06-20)

### Turn 219 [assistant] (2026-06-20)

### Turn 220 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Happy to unpack it — "ray-casting consensus" isn't a single named feature, it's the principle behind how grids decide to *clear* a cell. Let me build it up from the mechanic.

## First: what ray-casting (raytracing) is

When a lidar beam returns a distance, the grid does two things along that one beam:
- the **endpoint** cell (where the beam hit something) gets a **"hit"** → evidence it's *occupied*
- **every cell the beam passed through** on the way there gets a **"miss"** → evidence it's *free* (the beam traveled through it, so nothing was there)

That second part is "ray-casting to clear": a beam passing *through* a cell that the map currently calls occupied is evidence that the obstacle is gone. This is how a map clears a disappeared object.

## Why one cast is dangerous

A single beam passing through can be a lie: a reflection off glass, a missed/dropped return, a moment when the real obstacle was occluded by something thin, sensor noise. If you **clear on the first miss**, one bad beam deletes a real wall — and now the planner routes the robot into it. That's the asymmetry from before.

## "Consensus" = don't act on one observation; require agreement

There are three forms, and you'll meet all of them:

**1. Temporal / probabilistic consensus — log-odds (the important one).**
Each cell stores an *accumulated* occupancy score, not a yes/no. Every hit adds a positive amount, every miss adds a negative amount, and the cell only flips state when the **accumulated** evidence crosses a threshold. Worked example (illustrative numbers):

```
hit  = +0.85,  miss = −0.40,  "occ
...[clipped 3092 chars]

"Lidar–vision fusion" is a spectrum, not one thing — and the useful insight up front is **you're already doing the cheap end of it**, and the expensive end has a specific hardware path on your robot. Let me lay out the levels, then what's actually feasible on a Pi 4 + OAK-D.

## What fusion means — three levels

| Level | What gets combined | Example on your robot |
|---|---|---|
| **Early / low-level** (raw) | Raw point clouds or lidar points projected into the camera image | Merge OAK depth cloud + lidar points into one cloud; or color lidar points with RGB pixels |
| **Mid-level** (derived measurements) | Each sensor → a comparable intermediate, then merge | **Your depth→scan→costmap pipeline.** Collapse depth to a 2D scan, feed it alongside lidar `/scan` |
| **Late / high-level** (semantics) | Each sensor produces *detections*, then associate them | Camera NN says "glass door / person here," lidar says "geometry here," fuse into a labeled obstacle |

You already run **mid-level fusion**: `oakd/scan` is a second costmap obstacle source next to lidar `scan`, and your planned `depth_lidar_fusion` (inject depth into `/scan`) is mid-level too. So "can we do it?" — for geometry, **you've done it and validated ~16 ms/frame.**

## What fusion *requires* (the four things that make or break it)

1. **Extrinsic calibration** — the rigid transform between lidar frame and camera frame. You get a *rough* one for free from the TB4 URDF (factory mount positions), which is good enough for **costmap-level** fusion. **Pixel-accurate** RGB↔lidar projection (early fusion) needs a real calib

### Turn 224 [user] (2026-06-20)
so what are the advantages of lidar vision fusion

### Turn 225 [user] (2026-06-20)
so what are the advantages of lidar vision fusion for localization

### Turn 226 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
[thinking omitted]

### Turn 227 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
The advantages are best understood as **"vision fills exactly the holes where lidar localization fails."** Lidar-only localization (your AMCL/slam_toolbox setup) is precise and cheap *most* of the time — fusion earns its keep specifically in lidar's blind spots. Here are those blind spots and what vision adds to each.

## Lidar's localization failure modes → what vision fixes

**1. Perceptual aliasing (the big one).**
A long featureless corridor or a symmetric room produces a lidar scan that looks *identical* at many positions. The particle filter has no way to tell where along the corridor it is → the pose **slips/drifts along the ambiguous axis**. Vision breaks the tie: posters, doors, signage, wall texture are all *visually distinct* even when the geometry isn't. This is the single biggest reason to add vision to localization.

**2. Global relocalization / "kidnapped robot."**
If localization is lost (robot moved while off, or AMCL diverges), recovering from lidar alone is hard in repetitive buildings. **Visual place recognition** (appearance-based "I've seen this exact view before") relocalizes from scratch far more reliably. Cameras are simply better at "where am I, globally?"

**3. Semantic landmarks — and this one is tailor-made for a *guide* robot.**
A camera can read **room-number signs, doors, fire extinguishers, exit signs** — stable, *uniquely identifiable* anchors. Your robots literally navigate to numbered rooms (468, 436). Semantic visual localization lets the robot anchor its pose to "I see door 468," which lidar geometry can never give you. For a building g
...[clipped 2650 chars]

### Turn 228 [user] (2026-06-20)
how does camera emit the detections

### Turn 229 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
[thinking omitted]

### Turn 230 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Good — this is the mechanism behind the "let the camera do the NN" claim. The key idea: **the inference runs on the camera's VPU, and only the compact *results* cross USB** — never the heavy thing. Here's the chain.

## 1. The work happens on-device (the OAK-D pipeline)

The OAK-D has an Intel **Myriad X VPU**. You don't stream images to the Pi and run a model there — instead you upload a small **pipeline** (a DAG of processing nodes) that runs *on the camera*:

```
Camera sensor → resize/format → Neural Network node → (decode boxes) → XLinkOut → host
                                      ↑
                              compiled model (.blob)
```

- The model is an OpenVINO network **compiled to a `.blob`** for the Myriad X.
- A **detection** network (MobileNet-SSD, YOLO, etc.) decodes raw NN output into **bounding boxes on-chip** — so what leaves the device is already "class + confidence + box," not a tensor.
- **XLink** (the USB transport layer) ships that small result struct to the host. A few detections is bytes/frame, vs megabytes for a frame — that's why this is bandwidth-friendly and keeps your Pi 4 free.

## 2. The 3D part — *spatial* detections (this is the one you want)

The OAK can go one step further: a **SpatialDetectionNetwork** node fuses each 2D box with the **stereo depth** map and emits a **metric 3D position** for each detection — `(x, y, z)` in meters, in the camera optical frame. So instead of "there's a door somewhere in the image," you get "door at (1.2, −0.3, 2.4) m." *That* is what makes it usable for fusion/localization — it's already a 3D landmark
...[clipped 2525 chars]

## What's available *offline* on robot 468

Three models are pre-downloaded as Myriad X blobs in `depthai_examples/resources/` — these run with **no internet** and no blobconverter step:

| Model | Config | Type | Classes | Use for you |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| **MobileNet-SSD** | `nn/mobilenet.json` | object detection (2D or **spatial/3D**) | 20 (PASCAL VOC): person, chair, bottle, sofa, pottedplant, tvmonitor… | "person" → slow/yield near people; gives 3D position in spatial mode |
| **YOLOv4-tiny** | `nn/yolo.json` | object detection (2D or **spatial/3D**) | 80 (COCO): person, chair, bottle, backpack, cup, door**-less**… | broader classes, slightly heavier than MobileNet |
| **DeepLabv3+ (MobileNetV2)** | `nn/segmentation.json` | semantic **segmentation** (pixel mask) | 20 (VOC) | pixel-level masks; RGB-only, heaviest of the three |

Both detection nets support **spatial mode** — fused with your stereo depth they emit `SpatialDetectionArray` with a 3D `(x,y,z)` per object. The OAK-D-LITE has the same Myriad X VPU as the full OAK-D, so all three blobs run fine on it.

Anything beyond these three (DepthAI model zoo, a custom-trained "door/room-sign/glass" model) requires **internet** (blobconverter cache is empty) or an offline training+compile step — not available on the robot as-is.

## New status line

```

…and with subscription rate limits present:

```
ubuntu@pi4host:/home/ubuntu/cs7980-guide-mate ctx 45.2k/200k (23%) [5h:41% 7d:12%]
```

Colors: bold-green `user@host`, bold-blue path, dim-yellow `ctx`, dim-magenta `[5h/7d]`.

## What's behind each piece

- **Context usage** ✅ — uses the JSON's pre-computed `context_window` fields (`total_input_tokens`, `context_window_size`, `used_percentage`) rather than hand-parsing the transcript, so the numbers always match the actual API call.
- **Plan usage** ✅ *partially, better than I expected* — the payload **does** carry a `rate_limits` field for **Claude.ai Pro/Max subscribers**: `five_hour.used_percentage` and `seven_day.used_percentage`. Those are now shown as `[5h:N% 7d:N%]`. On an API-key plan (or if the field is absent) they're silently omitted.
- **Not available** ❌ — the `/usage` command's per-model token tallies, billing-cycle totals, and dollar amounts aren't in the hook payload, so those can't go in the status line.

## Caveat

Both `ctx ...` and `[5h/7d]` are **omitted until the first API response** of a session (the fields are `null` before then), so a brand-new session shows just `user@host:cwd` until the first turn completes. The script guards every field, so it never error

### Turn 273 [user] (2026-06-20)

### Turn 274 [assistant] (2026-06-20)

### Turn 275 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Two different questions hide in there, and they have opposite-sounding answers — both true. Here's the data we have:

- **Usage (undocked):** ~**−1.0 A** discharge (~14 W).
- **Charging (docked, net into battery):** +0.46 A at 24%, +0.35 A at 55% — and **dropping as it fills** (Li-ion CC-CV taper).

## Framing 1 — "On the dock, does charging beat usage?" → **Yes**

While docked, the dock powers the running electronics **directly** *and* tops up the battery at the same time. The battery current is **net positive** (+0.35–0.46 A), so the pack is *gaining* charge even while the full stack runs. On the dock it will always reach 100% and never deplete — charging outpaces usage. That's the number that matters for "can it run plugged in forever?" → yes.

## Framing 2 — "Does it fill faster than it drains?" → **No, slower**

If you compare raw rates:

| | Rate | Full traverse |
|---|---|---|
| Discharge (usage) | ~1.0 A | 1.97 Ah ÷ 1.0 A ≈ **~2 h** to empty |
| Charge (net) | ~0.35–0.46 A, tapering | ≈ **~4–5 h** to full |

So the battery **drains about 2–3× faster than it refills**. A ~2 h run takes ~4–5 h to put back. At 55% right now (0.88 Ah to go), with the current tapering toward 100%, you're looking at roughly 2.5+ hours to full and slowing near the top.

## Why both are true

The dock's *total* output ≈ the load (~14 W) **plus** the net charge (~0.4 A × ~15 V ≈ 6 W) ≈ **~20 W**. Most of the dock's power goes to *running* the robot; only the ~6 W remainder actually refills the battery. So it's always net-positive on the dock (Framing 1 wins), but the slice left over for char
...[clipped 387 chars]

### Turn 282 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
## Battery type

It's a **4-cell lithium-ion (Li-ion) pack** — the iRobot Create 3 base battery (Roomba-derived). From the live `battery_state` plus the spec:

- **Chemistry:** Li-ion
- **Configuration:** **4S** (4 cells in series). 14.4 V nominal ÷ 4 ≈ 3.6 V/cell; the 15.05 V we just read ÷ 4 ≈ 3.76 V/cell — textbook mid-charge Li-ion.
- **Capacity:** 1.967 Ah / **~28 Wh**, charged via the **dock contacts** only.

## Can you attach a powerbank? — short answer: not usefully, and here's the hard reason

There are only two things a powerbank could target, and both are blocked:

**1. Charge the Create 3 battery itself → No.**
The pack charges *only* through the dock's metal charging contacts at ~14.4 V+. There is **no USB charge-input** to the main battery, and a 5 V USB powerbank can't charge a 14.4 V Li-ion pack anyway (wrong voltage, no battery-management handshake). So you can't "top up the robot" from a USB brick.

**2. Power the Raspberry Pi directly → blocked by the wiring.**
This is the key constraint, confirmed in your own docs: the Pi and Create 3 are joined by **one USB-C cable that carries *both* power *and* the wired Ethernet link** (`192.168.186.0/24`, Pi=.3, Create3=.2). That cable occupies the Pi's USB-C power port. So:
- You can't add a powerbank to that port without **unplugging the cable that also carries the Create 3 ↔ Pi data link** — which would kill all base topics (odom, battery, motion, bumpers). The whole stack depends on that link.
- Powering the Pi separately would mean re-architecting the setup so power and the Create 3 Ethernet link come in on *di
...[clipped 1375 chars]

### Turn 338 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Short version: the **network and Pi are fine; the Create 3's DDS link to the Pi's discovery server dropped** — and the trigger was almost certainly the **low battery (19%) under teleop motor load**. Here's the reasoning.

## What actually broke (and what didn't)

- **Not the network** — ping is 5 ms, the Pi's own topics (`scan`, SLAM, map) are all alive. So WiFi/laptop/Pi are healthy.
- **What's gone is specifically the Create 3 base's participation in the ROS graph.** Every base topic (`battery_state`, `odom`, `cmd_vel`, `wheel_status`, `hazard_detection`, `dock`) vanished *together*. When one process's entire topic set disappears as a unit, that process (the Create 3's ROS interface) lost its discovery registration — it didn't crash individually per-topic.

This is the exact signature documented as **root cause #2 in `ros2-over-nuwave.md`**: the Create 3 loses contact with the Pi's FastDDS discovery server (`192.168.186.3:11811`) over the internal USB-C link, and all its participants time out and deregister.

## Why it happened *now* — the 19% battery is the smoking gun

The key architectural fact: **the USB-C cable between Create 3 and Pi carries *both* power *and* the `192.168.186.x` data link.** The Create 3 powers the Pi; that same cable is the base's only path to the discovery server.

Most likely chain:

1. You undocked for teleop and drove the battery down to **19%**.
2. At low state-of-charge, the pack voltage sags — and it sags *most* under **motor load** (exactly when teleop is driving the wheels).
3. That transient on the shared USB-C rail was enough to **glitc

### Turn 391 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Honest answer: I know **what** failed with high confidence from the logs, but the **why** is genuinely uncertain because the one log that would prove it — the Create 3's own app log — isn't exposed by this firmware (the 404 earlier).

## What failed (high confidence, from the kernel logs)

The Create 3's **ROS 2 application stopped/deregistered** — not the link, not power. The evidence rules out my earlier brownout guess:

- **`usb0` never disconnected** since the Jun 5 boot → the USB link between base and Pi was solid the whole time.
- **Pi under-voltage flag never tripped** (bit 16 = 0) → no power sag. (The throttling that did happen was *thermal*, not electrical.)
- **Web server + ping stayed up** throughout.
- Yet **all base ROS topics vanished at once** (`battery_state` publishers → 0, `odom`/`cmd_vel` gone).

### Turn 459 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Confirmed both answers from the actual code. Here's the picture:

## Will the BFS mapper use `scan_fused`?

**Indirectly yes — but only if you start the fusion node yourself first.** Two things to understand:

**1. The explorer never touches any scan.** `bfs_explorer` subscribes to exactly two topics (`bfs_explorer.py:105-110`): `map` (the SLAM occupancy grid) and `bump_points`. It plans BFS over the grid SLAM hands it. So it uses `scan_fused` *only* to the extent that **SLAM** is fed `scan_fused`.

**2. SLAM is wired to `scan_fused`, and so is Nav2** — that part is done:
- `config/slam_fused.yaml:19` → `scan_topic: scan_fused`
- `autonomous_mapping.launch.py:28` includes that file as SLAM's params
- `config/nav2_glass.yaml:164,213` → costmap obstacle source `topic: scan_fused`

**The gap (important):** `autonomous_mapping.launch.py` starts SLAM, Nav2, glass_guard, and the explorer — but it does **not** start the OAK camera or the `depth_lidar_fusion` node. So if you run only that launch file, SLAM subscribes to `scan_fused` **that nobody is publishing** → no scans → no map → the explorer waits forever on an empty grid. You must bring up the two producers first, in order:

```bash
# 1. camera (USB2) — see CLAUDE.md bring-up
ros2 run depthai_ros_driver camera_node --ros-args -r __ns:=/turtlebot468 -r __node:=oakd ... 
# 2. fusion node → publishes scan_fused
ros2 launch guide_mate_explorer depth_lidar_fusion.launch.py namespace:=turtlebot468
# 3. THEN the full stack
ros2 launch guide_mate_explorer autonomous_mapping.launch.py namespace:=turtlebot468
```

This matches the road

### Turn 522 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Done. Pushed as `3c1aaad`.

## What was broken
`autonomous_mapping.launch.py` pointed SLAM (`slam_fused.yaml`) and Nav2 (`nav2_glass.yaml`) at `scan_fused`, but **nothing in that launch published `scan_fused`** — no camera, no `depth_lidar_fusion`. So SLAM subscribed to an empty topic, the map never updated, and the explorer would wait forever on a blank grid. (That's the gap I flagged earlier.)

## The fixes

**1. Launch now starts the producers** — `camera_node → depth_lidar_fusion → SLAM + Nav2`, staggered with `TimerAction`s so each stage has its inputs ready:
- `camera` at t=0 (mirrors the known-good USB2 bring-up: `i_usb_speed:=HIGH`, `i_resolution:='400'`, NN off)
- `fusion` at t=6 (falls back to raw lidar until depth warms up, so SLAM gets scans immediately)
- `slam` + `nav2` at t=8
- `glass_guard` at t=14, `explorer` at t=22 (pushed back to let `map→odom` settle)
- New `start_camera:=false` arg so the documented manual camera bring-up still works without a double-launch USB conflict.

Validated with `ros2 launch --show-args` — parses cleanly, all includes (fusion/slam/nav2) resolve, `start_camera` exposed. No node code changed, so the symlink-installed launch is already live; no rebuild needed.

**2. RViz config** (`turtlebot468_mapping.rviz`) — added two displays so you can watch mapping progress from the laptop:
- **Frontiers / Goal (BFS)** — `/turtlebot468/frontier_markers` (the explorer's frontier clusters + current goal)
- **Nav2 Plan** — `/turtlebot468/plan` (cyan path to the chosen frontier)

Documented both in `docs/mapping/rviz-visualization.md`. I left th

### Turn 624 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Great question — and what we just hit (load 18 with the full stack) is exactly why this split matters. The short version: **the Pi runs only what physically owns the hardware; the laptop runs all the heavy compute.** This is actually the *intended* TurtleBot 4 design — TB4 is built to run SLAM/Nav2/RViz on a user PC, with the Pi as sensor+base bringup. We only crammed it all on the Pi for convenience, and it doesn't fit.

## What must stay on the robot (Pi) — hardware-bound, can't move
| Component | Why it's pinned to the Pi |
|---|---|
| **Create 3 base app** | Runs on the base board itself; owns motors, wheels, hazards, dock. |
| **RPLIDAR driver** (`rplidar_composition`) | Owns the USB lidar; publishes `/scan`. |
| **OAK-D camera driver** (`camera_node`) | Owns the USB camera; publishes depth. The *driver* must be local; the depth *processing* does not. |
| **FastDDS Discovery Server** | Already runs on the Pi; the laptop joins it as a super-client. |

These are cheap (drivers + base interface). The lidar + base + camera-driver together are well within the Pi's budget.

## What should move to the laptop — pure compute, just needs the topics
| Component | CPU we measured | Consumes → Produces |
|---|---|---|
| **slam_toolbox** | ~64% of a core (queue overflowed) | `scan_fused` → `/map` + `map→odom` |
| **Nav2** (planner/controller/bt_navigator/costmaps) | heavy once active | map+scan+TF → `cmd_vel` |
| **depth_lidar_fusion** | ~58% of a core | depth image + `/scan` → `scan_fused` |
| **bfs_explorer** | light | `/map` → nav goals |
| **glass_guard** | light | hazard/bump →
...[clipped 2130 chars]

| `cmd_vel`, TF | both | <1 Mbps |

- **Lidar-only SLAM+Nav2 in cloud:** ~1–2 Mbps total — trivially fits.
- **Including depth fusion in cloud:** ~40 Mbps up — **fits in 77 Mbps**, with headroom. So bandwidth-wise the *entire* stack (even depth) is feasible at these numbers.

### Turn 653 [assistant] (2026-06-20)

### Turn 654 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
**Yes — this network allows VPN, cleanly, on every protocol that matters.** Both TCP and UDP egress are open, and NAT traversal works:

| Protocol / port | Result |
|---|---|
| **WireGuard** UDP 51820 | ✅ UDP egress + NAT confirmed |
| **OpenVPN** UDP 1194 / TCP 1194 / TCP 443 | ✅ open |
| **IPSec/IKEv2** UDP 500 + 4500 (NAT-T) | ✅ open |
| **L2TP** 1701, **PPTP** 1723 | ✅ open (TCP) |
| **TLS/443** (universal fallback) | ✅ open |
| **UDP + NAT traversal** (STUN to Google) | ✅ **valid Binding Success response** — got our public XOR-mapped address back |

The STUN reply (`0101 000c 2112a442…` with the XOR-MAPPED-ADDRESS attribute) is the strong signal: arbitrary high-port **UDP gets out and NAT hole-punching works** — not just TCP. So you're not limited to VPN-over-TCP; native UDP tunnels work.

## What this means for the robot↔AWS link
- **WireGuard (UDP 51820) is the right choice** and will work natively here — lean, low-overhead, ideal for carrying ROS 2 DDS traffic. Pi gets a private tunnel IP (e.g. `10.8.0.2`), the EC2 instance `10.8.0.1`, and they're effectively on the same L3 subnet.
- **Why you need it for ROS 2 over AWS:** DDS discovery + traffic assumes mutually-routable peers; a WireGuard tunnel gives the robot and the cloud nodes a flat private subnet so DDS behaves like a LAN (you'd point `ROS_DISCOVERY_SERVER` / FastDDS at the tunnel IPs). This is the standard way to run cloud ROS 2.
- **Fallbacks exist** if campus policy ever tightens: TCP 443 is open, so WireGuard-over-TCP (`wstunnel`) or OpenVPN-TCP-443 would still get through.

These

### Turn 655 [user] (2026-06-20)
what is need of glass guard after lidar depth camera fusion

### Turn 656 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
[thinking omitted]

### Turn 657 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Because fusion and glass_guard defend against **different failure modes** — fusion is *predictive perception* (avoid before contact), glass_guard is the *contact-confirmed backstop* (never touch it twice). Fusion has real, known blind spots on the exact hazard it targets, and every one of them is a spot where only a bump will save you.

## What `depth_lidar_fusion` actually catches — and misses
Fusion folds OAK depth into the scan, but it only marks **what the depth camera can see**, which is narrower than you'd think:

| Fusion blind spot | Why it happens |
|---|---|
| **The clear pane itself** | Stereo depth fails on textureless transparent glass for the *same reason* lidar does — no return. Fusion catches the **metal base/frame** (~0.3–0.5 m), not the clean glass above it. A frameless or very clean pane can still ghost. |
| **Anything outside the camera FOV** | OAK is a ~70–90° forward wedge; the lidar is 360°. Glass approached from the side or sweeping in during a turn isn't in depth view. |
| **Near blind zone (<0.4 m) & beyond max range** | Robot nose-up to glass, or glass farther than depth range, isn't seen. |
| **Depth stalls (X_LINK)** | When the camera goes 0 Hz, fusion degrades to **raw lidar** — fully glass-blind until recovery. |
| **Bad lighting / reflections** | Sun glare, reflections, low texture all degrade stereo matching. |

So fusion **reduces** glass collisions; it doesn't eliminate them. It's one perception modality with documented failures on transparent surfaces.

## What `glass_guard` adds that fusion can't
- **Ground truth.** A Create 3 **bump** m

### Turn 668 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Reading the code, your instinct is confirmed — and the 28% is almost certainly **not** glass_guard's true steady-state cost. Here's what it actually does when idle:

- `_publish` at **5 Hz**: builds a PointCloud2 from `self.points` — which is **empty** until a bump happens (0 points) — plus a small marker. Cheap.
- `_backup_tick` at **20 Hz**: but it **early-returns immediately** when no back-up is active (`_backup_until is None`). A 20 Hz no-op.
- TF lookup / marking: only on an actual **bump event** (rare).

That should idle at **~1–3% of a core**, not 28%. So the snapshot was almost certainly **skewed**: `ps %CPU` is a *lifetime average*, and I sampled it shortly after glass_guard launched (it starts at t=14 in the run) **while the Pi was at load 10–18**. The one-time startup imports (`numpy`, `tf2_ros`, `rclpy`) plus scheduling contention get averaged over a short window → an inflated number that isn't the real steady state.

### Turn 689 [user] (2026-06-20)
can we combine all nodes in one? but keep the code moduler

### Turn 690 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
[thinking omitted]

### Turn 691 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Yes — this is a standard ROS 2 pattern, and it directly attacks that per-node tax. The trick is: **modularity lives in the code, not in the process count.** Each node stays its own class in its own file; you just add one launcher that runs them all in a single process under one executor.

## The pattern
```
guide_mate_explorer/
  bfs_explorer.py        # class BfsExplorer(Node)  + its own main()  ← unchanged, still runnable solo
  glass_guard.py         # class GlassGuard(Node)   + its own main()  ← unchanged
  depth_lidar_fusion.py  # class DepthLidarFusion(Node) + own main()  ← unchanged
  combined_node.py       # NEW: imports the classes, ONE executor, spins all
```
```python
# combined_node.py
def main():
    rclpy.init()
    ex = MultiThreadedExecutor()
    nodes = [BfsExplorer(), GlassGuard(), DepthLidarFusion()]
    for n in nodes:
        ex.add_node(n)
    try:
        ex.spin()
    finally:
        for n in nodes: n.destroy_node()
        rclpy.shutdown()
```
Plus one `console_scripts` entry (`guide_mate_bringup`). Each node keeps its standalone `main()`, so you can **still `ros2 run` them individually for debugging** — you've lost nothing, you've just added a combined entry point. The code is exactly as modular as before.

## What it actually saves — and the catch
**Saves (the overhead):** N separate executor spin-loops and N Python processes collapse to **one**. That spin/idle housekeeping was the bulk of glass_guard's 16%, so combining should bring the three nodes much closer to ~one node's overhead than to 3×. (How much DDS discovery cost also collapses depend
...[clipped 1626 chars]

### Turn 727 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
**Root cause confirmed.** Here's the full evidence chain:

| Probe | Result | Conclusion |
|---|---|---|
| Empty rclpy node (no listener) | **0.0%** | no fixed per-node/participant tax |
| Both nodes create `tf2_ros.TransformListener` | ✅ (glass_guard:73, bfs_explorer:119) | each parses `/tf` independently |
| `/tf` publish rate | **~31 Hz** | a busy stream (odom/base_link/wheels/casters) |
| 1 node | ~16% / 2 nodes | ~32% | linear — each listener re-does the same work |

So the 16%/node is **Python deserializing the 31 Hz `/tf` firehose in a `TransformListener`** — and each node runs its *own* listener, parsing the identical stream twice. The empty node proves it's not baseline overhead; the `/tf` rate + duplicate listeners explain the linear scaling.

**Which means the thing I built — combining into one *process* — was the wrong lever, and the measurement proved it (31.8% ≈ the sum, zero saving).** I owe you a straight account: my first guess (startup skew) was wrong, my second (per-participant DDS tax) was wrong, and only the empty-node + `/tf`-rate experiments pinned it. The real cost is **duplicated TF work**, and that doesn't care how many processes you use — it cares how many `TransformListener`s you run.

## The actual fix (and it *is* "combine into one," done right)
Reduce **listeners**, not processes: **one Node that owns a single `tf2_ros.Buffer` + `TransformListener`**, shared by both behaviors. Parse `/tf` once → **~16% total instead of 32%** (roughly halves it; add fusion's listener later and the saving compounds).

### Turn 734 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Confirmed, and this is the real insight:

| Node | Its only TF need | How often | What it actually does |
|---|---|---|---|
| **glass_guard** | `map → bumper` (to place a bump in the map) | **only on a physical bump** (rare — could be never in a run) | buffers `/tf` at **31 Hz, 24/7** |
| **bfs_explorer** | `map → base_link` (robot's pose to start BFS) | **1×/sec** (in `_tick`) | buffers `/tf` at **31 Hz**, uses ~1 of every ~31 |

So the honest answer to "why are they even listening to that": **they're not listening because they need the stream — they need a couple of tiny lookups.** They listen to the entire 31 Hz tree because that's the *only* thing `tf2_ros.TransformListener` knows how to do: subscribe to all of `/tf`, deserialize every transform, and buffer the whole tree so that *any* lookup at *any* recent timestamp can be interpolated. It's a general-purpose "buffer everything, always" design. For a node whose real need is "the latest robot pose, once a second" — or for glass_guard, "one lookup the next time I bump something" — that's wildly oversized. **The mismatch between what they buffer (everything, 31 Hz) and what they use (a trickle) *is* the 16%.**

glass_guard is the egregious one: it burns ~16% of a core **continuously** to be ready for a lookup that may not happen for the entire run.

## So the better fix than "combine nodes"
You've basically diagnosed it: don't right-size by *merging processes* — right-size the **TF consumption**:

1. **glass_guard shouldn't hold a standing listener at all.** Its `bumper → base_link` part is **static** (`/tf_static`, ~0 H
...[clipped 962 chars]

### Turn 737 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Yeah — let's reason about it, because your instinct is half-right and the *why-not* is the interesting part.

**The need genuinely is low-frequency.** bfs_explorer wants "where am I" once a second; glass_guard wants one transform the instant it bumps. Neither needs 31 Hz freshness. So conceptually, if they consumed TF at 2–3 Hz they'd be perfectly happy, and the cost would drop roughly proportionally (the 16% is mostly *Python deserializing each `/tf` message* — fewer messages, less CPU, near-linearly). So in principle, yes, lower frequency would fix it.

**The catch is there's no "sample slower" knob on the listener.** `tf2_ros.TransformListener` is built to subscribe to `/tf` and ingest *every* message so its buffer can interpolate a lookup at *any* timestamp. It has no "only process every Nth" setting. You give it the topic and it drinks the whole firehose. The `Buffer(cache_time=…)` parameter controls how *long* it remembers, not how *fast* it samples — so that doesn't help.

**You also can't just turn down `/tf` at the source.** That 31 Hz is published by the base for *everyone* — Nav2's controller and SLAM need high-rate, fresh pose to localize and steer accurately. `/tf` is a shared resource sized for the *hungriest* consumer. Slowing it to please our two cheap nodes would blur localization for the whole stack. Bad trade.

**And throttling `/tf` as a topic is a known foot-gun.** `/tf` interleaves many independent frame-pairs (odom→base, base→wheels, map→odom…) in one stream. A naive throttle (`topic_tools throttle`) drops messages blindly, so you randomly starve some
...[clipped 1447 chars]

### Turn 738 [user] (2026-06-20)
canwe safely create c++ nodes and run them with python nodes?

### Turn 739 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
[thinking omitted]

### Turn 740 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Yes — completely safe and completely standard. This is one of ROS 2's core design points, not a hack.

**Why it just works:** rclcpp (C++) and rclpy (Python) are both thin wrappers over the *same* underlying rcl/rmw/DDS core. Nodes never talk "Python to Python" or "C++ to C++" — they talk **DDS to DDS**, exchanging messages in the same serialized wire format (CDR) regardless of source language. A C++ publisher and a Python subscriber on the same topic are indistinguishable from two Python nodes. The language is invisible at the communication layer. Same for services, actions, parameters, and **TF** (a C++ node's transforms land on `/tf` and your Python listeners read them fine, and vice-versa).

**How you run them:** each node is just its own process/executable. A C++ node compiles to a binary; you `ros2 run` it or drop a `Node(...)` action in the *same* launch file next to the Python ones — launch doesn't care what language an executable is. No bridge, no shim (that ROS1↔ROS2 pain doesn't exist here). So "run C++ and Python together" literally means: start both. They discover each other over DDS like everything else.

**The few things to actually get right** (none are C++/Python-specific, they're the usual ROS 2 hygiene):
- **QoS must match** between a pub and its sub — e.g., our depth images are BEST_EFFORT, so a C++ subscriber has to request best-effort too, exactly as the Python side does today.
- **Message types must match** — use the same `.msg` definitions (standard `sensor_msgs`, etc.), which they already are.
- **Build system** — a C++ node is an `ament_cmake` pack
...[clipped 1392 chars]

### Turn 806 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Good question to ask now, because we've measured exactly where the cost lives. First, the inventory — **everything except our three custom nodes is already C++** (slam_toolbox, all of Nav2, the rplidar driver, the depthai camera driver). So "move to C++" only applies to *our* code, which is:

| Our node | What it costs | C++ candidate? |
|---|---|---|
| `depth_lidar_fusion` | per-frame depth math + TF (the 58%) | ✅ **in progress** |
| `bfs_explorer` | TF listener (16%) **+ BFS flood-fill that grows with the map** | ✅ **strong — do next** |
| `glass_guard` | ~16%, almost entirely the TF listener; logic is trivial | ⚠️ optional |
| `combined_node` | just a launcher | n/a |

## `bfs_explorer` — the strongest next port
It has *two* CPU-bound costs, and one of them gets worse exactly when you can least afford it:
- **The BFS itself.** It runs two flood-fills over the whole occupancy grid every 1 s, in pure Python. A 30 m × 30 m map at 5 cm = 360k cells; that's hundreds of thousands of cell-ops/sec interpreted — and it **scales with map size**, so it's cheapest at the start of a run and most expensive late, when SLAM is also hammering the Pi. In C++ that flood-fill is sub-millisecond and basically free. This is the single best argument for porting it.
- **The TF listener** (the same 16% we found) — cheap in C++.
- Risk is *lower* than fusion: a frontier-selection bug just wastes a trip, it doesn't corrupt the SLAM map. The extra work is porting the Nav2 action client + frontier clustering (mechanical, more lines than fusion).

## `glass_guard` — optional, and maybe not worth a re
...[clipped 1863 chars]

### Turn 1216 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
Benchmark **complete** — and the results are decisive. (The `Killed` lines are just per-run teardown, not errors.)

## C++ vs Python — steady-state CPU under identical synthetic load (isolated domain, no robot)

| Node | Python | C++ | Speedup | Load tested |
|---|---:|---:|:---:|---|
| **depth_lidar_fusion** | **52.4%** | **3.7%** | **~14×** | 640×480 depth @6 Hz + scan @8 Hz + tf @31 Hz |
| **bfs_explorer** | **95.8%** | **5.7%** | **~17×** | 160k-cell map, replan @2 Hz + tf @31 Hz |
| **glass_guard** | **17.8%** | **1.8%** | **~10×** | idle (tf @31 Hz parse only) |

Health-line counts confirm both versions were genuinely working (fusion: both resolved extrinsics + processed depth; bfs: C++ logged **71** progress cycles vs Python's 3 in the same window — it's so much faster it replanned far more often; glass: both just idle-parsing TF).

## What this means
- **Aggregate: Python 3-node stack = 166% of a core (1.66 cores); C++ as separate processes = 11.2%.** That's the ~15× that turns "the Pi melts at load 18" into "comfortably fits."
- It confirms the predictions exactly: the fusion ~14× matches CLAUDE.md's "~10× cheaper"; glass_guard's 17.8%→1.8% is precisely the TF-parse tax we diagnosed; bfs's Python **95.8%** (nearly a full core saturated by the pure-Python flood-fill over 160k cells) → 5.7% validates why the BFS was the strongest C++ candidate.
- The **container** goes further still: those 3 C++ processes each carry a ~1.8% TF parse; sharing one listener collapses that to a single parse (idle container measured at 1.5%), so under load the container ≈ fusion+bfs work

### Turn 1263 [assistant] (2026-06-20)
I checked the actual code. Here's the accurate picture.

## 1. bfs_explorer does NOT send cmd_vel

You're right that it *shouldn't*, and it doesn't. It's already exactly the goal-level loop you described:

- subscribes to `/map`, runs BFS to the nearest frontier,
- calls `sendGoal()` → one `NavigateToPose` action goal to Nav2,
- **waits** for the result in `goalResult` (SUCCEEDED → replan; aborted/timeout → blacklist that spot → replan).

It never publishes a `Twist`. The `cmd_vel` on `/turtlebot468/cmd_vel` is produced by **Nav2** (`controller_server` → `velocity_smoother`), not by the explorer. So there is nothing to fix in bfs on that front — the only node in our package that can emit `cmd_vel` directly is `glass_guard`, and only if its optional `reactive_backup` param is true (it defaults **false**, and we keep it false).

## 2. Can we unsubscribe TF in bfs and just use goals + maps?

Not entirely — and here's the catch. bfs uses TF (`map → base_link`) in two places:

- **`robotCell()` (line 152): essential.** BFS finds the *nearest reachable* frontier, so it needs a **start cell** = where the robot currently is on the map. That comes from the `map → base_link` lookup. Drop it and there's no seed for the search — the core algorithm breaks.
- **`sendGoal()` (line 402): cosmetic.** Just computes the goal yaw so the robot faces the frontier; already falls back to `yaw = 0` on failure. This one we *could* drop with no real loss.

So "just depend on nav goals and maps" can't fully replace TF: maps tell you *where the frontiers are*, but not *where the robot is*. You need a r
...[clipped 1336 chars]

Read it all — `docs/camera.md`, `scripts/` (+ README), the new `oak-camera-usbfs-rootcause` memory, and confirmed the live state. Here's my understanding and where it stands.

## Camera: root-caused, fixed, currently streaming

**Two distinct failures** (and the *old* "power starvation → force USB2 → need a Y-adapter" / "CPU saturation" explanations were both **wrong** — under-voltage never once latched):

1. **The daily wedge** = kernel `usbfs_memory_mb=16` is too small for the OAK's USB bulk transfers → `X_LINK_ERROR` on the stereo stream → driver aborts ~5 s after "Camera ready", and the TB4 container has no respawn so it sits dead at bootloader (`2485`). **Fix: `usbfs_memory_mb=256`** — the *exact same* SUPER/USB3 config then runs indefinitely. (Forcing USB2 only *delayed* the crash, which is why USB2 "felt" stable.)
2. **Residual runtime drop** = a current-draw **brownout on the camera's own 5 V rail**, and *only* in one mode: **heavy RGB+depth on USB3**. Proven not-CPU / not-temp / not-Pi-rail by the mode matrix + load sweep (depth-only survived a fully saturated, thermally-throttling Pi with 0 drops). True fix is hardware power; software mitigation is depth-only + watchdog.

**Stable config = depth-only + USB2 + `usbfs=256`**, and the full mapping stack alongside it is proven safe. Mapping needs **neither** the OAK IMU (SLAM uses `scan_fused` + Create 3 odom) **nor** RGB (fusion consumes depth only).

**Live state right now — it's actually up and healthy:**
- `lsusb` → `03e7:f63b` (firmware streaming, not bootloader)
- `usbfs_memory_mb=256` at runtime **and** persist

## Pushed
`5d88da0..6b8c7b8` → `origin/docs/aws-iot-core` (working tree clean, in sync). One comprehensive commit covering all outstanding work.

## What got documented & committed
**Code**
- `src/guide_mate_perception/` — the C++ package: ports of all three nodes + the `guide_mate_container` (shared single `/tf` listener), with a new **README** carrying the benchmark matrix (~10–17× cheaper; aggregate Python ~166% → C++ ~11% of a core).
- `config/nav2_no_motion.yaml` — every Nav2 velocity zeroed (smoother `[0,0,0]`, FollowPath caps 0, recovery cut to `[spin, wait]`) so the full stack runs on a docked robot that can't move.
- The combined `guide_mate_bringup` Python runner (`combined_node.py` + launch + setup.py entry).

**Docs**
- `docs/mapping/bringup-no-motion.md` (new) — the no-motion procedure, the `auto_standby` lidar-wake mechanism, and the **Claude-shell DDS-isolation** finding (Bash-tool nodes aren't cross-discovered by the boot systemd nodes → can't wake the lidar → run from the robot's terminal).
